   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d190594217" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Prussian blue (PB) is regarded as a promising cathode for sodium-ion batteries because of its sustainable precursor elements (e.g., Mn, Fe), easy preparation, and unique framework structure. However, the unstable structure and inherent crystal H<sub>2</sub>O restrain its practical application. For this purpose, a self-constructed trace Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doped PB prepared via a sea-water-mediated method is proposed to address this problem. The Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping in the Na sites of PB is permitted by both thermodynamics and kinetics factors when synthesized in sea water. The results reveal that the introduced Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> are immovable in the PB lattices and can form stronger K‒N and Mg‒N Coulombic attraction to relieve phase transition and element dissolution. Besides, the Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping can reduce defect and H<sub>2</sub>O contents. As a result, the PB prepared in sea water exhibits an extremely long cycle life (80.1% retention after 2400 cycles) and superior rate capability (90.4% capacity retention at 20 C relative to that at 0.1 C). To address its practical applications, a sodium salts recycling strategy is proposed to greatly reduce the PB production cost. This work provides a self-constructed Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doped high-performance PB at a low preparation cost for sustainable, large-scale energy storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs9580-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs9580-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium ion batteries are considered as the next generation electrochemical devices for large-scale energy storage because of low cost, abundant resources, environmental friendliness, and high safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0001_R_d190594207e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0005_R_d190594207e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Engineering">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The performance of the cathode materials plays a key role in realizing a stable and high-efficiency energy storage. Among various cathode materials, Prussian blue (PB) materials with a rigid and open structure that ensures rapid Na<sup>+</sup> mobility and adaptive volume variation have received enormous interests and in recent years, there are some companies and researchers focusing on the PB technology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0006_R_d190594207e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Power Sources">6</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0010_R_d190594207e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nanoscale Horiz.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The general formula of Na-based PB can be described as Na<sub>x</sub>M<sub>1</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1−y</sub>□<sub>y</sub>·nH<sub>2</sub>O, where M<sub>1</sub> are the transition metals and □ represents the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancy (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 2, 0 ≤ <i>y</i> &lt; 1).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0011_R_d190594207e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - iScience">11</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0013_R_d190594207e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As previously reported, the rational selection of transition metals in M<sub>1</sub> site can realize either high energy density (M<sub>1</sub> = Mn) or long cycle life (M<sub>1</sub> = Ni, Fe), showing a great potential in large-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0014_R_d190594207e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0019_R_d190594207e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Recently, Na site doping strategy with trace alkali metal ions and alkali earth metal ions was reported to effectively improve electrochemical performance of PB by reducing H<sub>2</sub>O content to stabilize the framework structure and to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> migration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0020_R_d190594207e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0021_R_d190594207e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, for large-scale preparation of PB, besides electrochemical performance, rational selections of precursors and preparation routes should also be taken into consideration. It is noted that the preparation of PB would consume a lot of deionized (DI) water which is environmentally and economically unfriendly, and that the ion doping would add the production cost and complicate the preparation process. In addition, the synthesis of PB needs a lot of chelating agents and auxiliary salts in order to control the co-precipitation reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0017_R_d190594207e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Chem. A">17</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0022_R_d190594207e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0023_R_d190594207e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Without them, the co-precipitation reactions would become uncontrollable, producing irregular, tiny particles with substantial [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4‒</sup> vacancy and crystal water.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0023_R_d190594207e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0028_R_d190594207e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A low-cost, sustainable preparation method is thereby necessary to realize the practical applications of PB.</p>
         
         <p>It seems that the low-grade water, e.g. sea water, can directly be used to synthesize PB materials. Potential doping ions such as Li<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, and Al<sup>3+</sup> can be found in some low-grade water to realize natural doping, and the use of low-grade water can achieve goal of low-cost and sustainable preparation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0029_R_d190594207e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano Energy">29</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0031_R_d190594207e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is expected that the production cost could be further reduced by recycling and repeated use of the chelating agents and auxiliary salts since their chemical composition and structure are usually kept intact during synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0032_R_d190594207e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">32</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0033_R_d190594207e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the practical applications of PB, it is critical to realize both performance improvement and sustainable preparation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0017_R_d190594207e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Chem. A">17</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0022_R_d190594207e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0026_R_d190594207e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0034_R_d190594207e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Mater. Today Sustain.">34</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0037_R_d190594207e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Cent. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Inspired by this, in this work, we propose a promising strategy to use low-grade water to prepare PB that realizes natural ion doping and low preparation cost. The morphology, structure, composition, and electrochemical performance of the PB materials prepared in different water were systematically studied. The ability of ions intercalation into the PB synthesized in different water was investigated from both thermodynamics and kinetics aspects. The results show that the preparation of PB in sea water can self-construct Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping in the Na sites that significantly enhances the cycling stability (80.1% capacity retention after 2400 cycles). Theoretical calculation reveals the intrinsic mechanism for structural stabilization of the PB framework by Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping. In addition, we propose a sodium salts (chelating agent and auxiliary salt) recycling method to further address cost-effective and sustainable preparation of PB.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs9580-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs9580-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs9580-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation and Structural Analyses of the PB Materials</h3>
            
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of the sea water sample (SW-PB) is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs9580-fig-0001">1a</a>. It can be seen that the particles prepared in sea water exhibit an aggregation morphology which is built by several cubic particles with a size of 100‒150&nbsp;nm. Such a growth mode not only increases the particle size which is favorable for restraining particle dissolution, increasing energy density, and improving electrode processing, but also forms a multi-edge structure to improve Na-ion diffusion at the electrode/electrolyte interface. Similar morphology is observed in the other PB samples prepared in DI water (DW-PB), lake water (LW-PB), and tap water (TW-PB) as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a–S3a</a> (Supporting Information). It seems that the particle size of SW-PB is smaller than that of DW-PB, LW-PB and TW-PB. The particle size distribution exhibits that the <i>D</i><sub>50</sub> values of SW-PB, DW-PB, LW-PB, and TW-PB are 441, 620, 769, and 700&nbsp;nm, respectively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0001">1b</a>, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b–S3b</a> (Supporting Information). The unique morphology and small size of SW-PB are beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and capacity release.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5480aab2-39a5-4a63-afb0-78f29ce4a104/advs9580-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5480aab2-39a5-4a63-afb0-78f29ce4a104/advs9580-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/087caf98-ee71-46a6-aedc-65112d9eb581/advs9580-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5480aab2-39a5-4a63-afb0-78f29ce4a104/advs9580-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM image, b) particle size distribution, c) Rietveld refined XRD patterns and d) TG of SW-PB. e) Schematic illustration of composition, structure and morphology of the PB prepared in different water. f) TEM image, SAED patterns, HAADF-STEM image and EDX mapping of SW-PB.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) Rietveld refinements were performed to investigate the influence of water quality on crystal structure of the PB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c–S3c</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Note that the PB materials prepared in different water all exhibit a monoclinic structure (space group: P2<sub>1</sub>/n), suggesting a high Na content in the lattices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0038_R_d190594207e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0039_R_d190594207e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small Methods">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Clearly, DW-PB, LW-PB, and TW-PB show similar crystal parameters while SW-PB exhibits a reduced crystal volume and a regulated <i>β</i> angle (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The optimized framework structure and <i>β</i> angle can adapt the bond angle changes during phase transition from monoclinic tο&nbsp;cubic and even to tetragonal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0022_R_d190594207e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0040_R_d190594207e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0041_R_d190594207e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) analysis reveals there are trace Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> in SW-PB, but the compositions of LW-PB and TW-PB are similar as DW-PB (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In order to reveal the effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping on crystal growth, we prepared a series of PB samples in solutions with a gradient Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> concentration. Obviously, the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> does have a structure regulating effect, where the PB exhibits a notable size decrease tendency as Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> concentration increases (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, the lattice parameter evolution also demonstrates a structure regulating effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, which is consistent with the tendency of the sea water synthesis (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6–S8</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The above results suggest that Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping indeed has a positive effect on morphology and structure regulation.</p>
            
            <p>Thermogravimetric (TG) results show that the H<sub>2</sub>O content of SW-PB is 10.87 wt.%, obviously lower than that of DW-PB (12.04%), LW-PB (11.90%) and TW-PB (12.74%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d–S3d</a>, Supporting Information). The reduced water content in the PB can decrease irreversible side reactions during cycling. To clarify the reason for crystal H<sub>2</sub>O reduction after Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> introduction, TG test was also performed on the PB samples synthesized in solutions with different Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> concentrations (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). It can be seen that K+ doping helps to remove crystal H<sub>2</sub>O, while Mg<sup>2+</sup> doping has an opposite effect. The large ionic size of K<sup>+</sup> could restrain the occupation of interstitial H<sub>2</sub>O in PB framework as previously reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0013_R_d190594207e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0020_R_d190594207e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0042_R_d190594207e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0043_R_d190594207e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Res.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The difference in composition, structure, and morphology between the PB prepared in fresh water (DI water, lake water and tap water) and sea water is schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0001">1e</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0001">1f</a> shows the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image, selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns, high-angle annular dark field (HAADF)-scanning TEM (STEM) image, and the corresponding energy dispersive X-ray spectrometry (EDX) mapping. It is evident that uniform Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> doping is realized in SW-PB with a monoclinic structure.</p>
            
            <p>Water quality analysis was performed to clarify the intrinsic mechanism as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). Note that alkali or alkaline earth metal ions (Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>) constitute the main impurity ions in the low-grade water, while the contents of transition metal ions such as Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> are negligible. Therefore, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> doping in the Na sites of PB is expected. In order to illustrate the intercalation ability and occupation of Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> in the PB framework, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted as shown <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0002">2</a>. As previously reported,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0009_R_d190594207e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0034_R_d190594207e911" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Mater. Today Sustain.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> there are four possible interstitial sites in PB framework with Wyckoff notations of 8c (body center), 24d (face-center), 32f(c) (displaced from 8c sites toward the C-coordinated corner), and 32f(n) (displaced from 8c sites toward the N-coordinated corner) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0002">2a</a>. The calculated ions insertion energy at different interstitial sites reveals that Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and Ca<sup>2+</sup> with a small ionic radius of 1.02 Å, 0.72 Å, and 1.00 Å prefer to occupy the 24d sites while the K<sup>+</sup> with a larger ionic radius of 1.38 Å is more stable at the 8c sites. The insertion energy of the alkali or alkaline earth metal ions into the MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework follows the order of <i>E</i><sub>i1</sub>(Ca<sup>2+</sup>, 24d) &lt; <i>E</i><sub>i1</sub>(K<sup>+</sup>, 8c) ≈ <i>E</i><sub>i1</sub>(Mg<sup>2+</sup>, 24d) &lt; <i>E</i><sub>i1</sub>(Na<sup>+</sup>, 24d), suggesting that the intercalation of Ca<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Mg<sup>2+</sup> into MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework is thermodynamically favorable in the Na<sup>+</sup>-free PB framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0002">2b</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8cea325d-1be5-47e5-911c-b184d92b818f/advs9580-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8cea325d-1be5-47e5-911c-b184d92b818f/advs9580-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/874062ed-5e1a-467b-babf-41be4f1010ca/advs9580-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8cea325d-1be5-47e5-911c-b184d92b818f/advs9580-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Crystal structure of MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> with possible interstitial sites and the corresponding primitive cells with alkali or alkaline earth metal ions occupying at 8c, 24d, 32f(n), and 32f(c) sites. The calculated intercalation energy of Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Ca<sup>2+</sup> into b) MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and c) NaMnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Besides, the insertion energy of Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Ca<sup>2+</sup> into the Na<sup>+</sup>-rich PB framework was also calculated since the majority of the interstitial sites during practical synthesis would be occupied by Na<sup>+</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0002">2c</a>. The insertion energy for Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> into the NaMnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework follows the order of <i>E</i><sub>i2</sub>(Na<sup>+</sup>, 24d) ≈ <i>E</i><sub>i2</sub>(K<sup>+</sup>, 24d/8c) &lt; <i>E</i><sub>i2</sub>(Ca<sup>2+</sup>, 24d) &lt;&lt; <i>E</i><sub>i2</sub>(Mg<sup>2+</sup>, 24d), suggesting that the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> is thermodynamically preferential in the whole process. Of note is that the initial occupied Na<sup>+</sup> in NaMnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework is fixed at 24d site because of the lowest thermodynamic energy. Although the intercalation of Ca<sup>2+</sup> into NaMnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> at 24d site is also energetically permitted, its insertion is more difficult than Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> which are energetically more favorable. For Mg<sup>2+</sup>, the intercalation into the NaMnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework is thermodynamically unfavorable. This indicates that introduction of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Ca<sup>2+</sup> only occurs in the initial stage. The detailed insertion energy for Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Ca<sup>2+</sup> into MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework at different sites is summarized at Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information). Besides thermodynamics, the kinetics aspect should also be considered since there is always competition for intercalation between the ions arising from concentration difference. For PB synthesis, the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration is usually close to its saturation value (over 160&nbsp;g L<sup>‒1</sup> in this work) which is much higher than those of the other ions. In contrast, the intercalation of Ca<sup>2+</sup> is kinetically shielded because of its rather low concentration (315.55&nbsp;mg L<sup>‒1</sup>) in sea water. The low concentration of the impurity ions in lake water and tap water also makes their insertion kinetically impossible. But in sea water, the shielding effect of Na<sup>+</sup> is greatly weakened since the K<sup>+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> have a relatively higher concentration of 582.89 and 1001.29&nbsp;mg L<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively, so as to realize K<sup>+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> intercalation. As a result, a self-construction of K<sup>+</sup>/Mg<sup>2+</sup> co-doping in the PB was achieved by synthesizing it in sea water.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs9580-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance and Mechanism of the PB Materials</h3>
            
            <p>Electrochemical performance of SW-PB, DW-PB, TW-PB, and LW-PB was investigated by galvanostatic cycling in a voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs9580-fig-0003">3</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11‒S13</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that SW-PB delivers a comparable discharge capacity of 115.8 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1 C as DW-PB, TW-PB, and LW-PB (121.5, 118.5, and 118.5 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively). Based on the chemical formula of SW-PB, the theory discharge capacity contributed by Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> is 4.8 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0043_R_d190594207e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Res.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is close to the decreased value compared with DW-PB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0003">3a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> may stay in SW-PB lattice during charge. In order to prove this assumption, elemental analysis of Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Mg<sup>+</sup> in SW-PB at pristine and charge state was carried out (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Note that the Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> can well stay in the interstitial sites upon charge to support structural stability of the PB and the capacity comes mainly from extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. The residual Na<sup>+</sup> at charge state is due to the doping of inert Ni<sup>2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0017_R_d190594207e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Chem. A">17</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0044_R_d190594207e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0045_R_d190594207e1192" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although there is possible blocking effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup>and K<sup>+</sup> for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, SW-PB exhibits superior rate performance with 90.4% capacity retained at 20 C relative to that at 0.1 C.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ba8d135d-fd32-4c64-857f-7317cc8ba27a/advs9580-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ba8d135d-fd32-4c64-857f-7317cc8ba27a/advs9580-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6a8d4a55-f913-4e47-bdf5-48fe9f10a54b/advs9580-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ba8d135d-fd32-4c64-857f-7317cc8ba27a/advs9580-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of SW-PB. a) Voltage curves in the initial three cycles at 0.1 C. b) Discharge curves and c) discharge capacity at various current densities from 0.1 C to 20 C, and d) cycling stability at 5 C. The migration models for e) Na<sup>+</sup>, f) Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and g) K<sup>+</sup> to move from one interstitial site ([A] site) to adjacent interstitial site ([B] site) and h) the corresponding migration energy barriers for Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup> to move from A site to B site.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Cycling performance of the four PB samples is compared as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11d–S13d</a> (Supporting Information). Note that SW-PB with Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping exhibits the best cycling stability with a 93.9% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 5 C, superior to DW-PB (88.6%), LW-PB (88.4%) and TW-PB (87.6%). Even after 2400 cycles at 5 C, SW-PB can still maintain a high capacity retention of 80.1%. The significantly enhanced cycling stability for SW-PB indicates that the introduced Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> in the PB can act as “pillar” to well stabilize the structure during repeated cycling. In order to further illustrate the “pillar” effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> in SW-PB, the migration energy barriers (<i>E</i><sub>m</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Mg<sup>2+</sup> in PB framework (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0003">3e‒h</a>) was calculated through DFT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0046_R_d190594207e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Res.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The migration energy required for Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup> to move from A site to B site in PB framework is 0.17&nbsp;eV, 5.70&nbsp;eV, and 0.44&nbsp;eV, respectively. Clearly, the migration energy barriers of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> (especially for Mg<sup>2+</sup>) in the PB is obviously larger than that of Na<sup>+</sup> because of the higher charge density of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and larger ionic size of K<sup>+</sup>. The immovability of K<sup>+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> upon cycling makes them act as the “pillar” to well support the PB framework, thus significantly enhancing cycling performance.</p>
            
            <p>In order to reveal the impacts of impurities such as Al<sup>3+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup> and organic substances on electrochemical performance of SW-PB, we also tested the electrochemical performance of the PB directly synthesized in solution with comparable Mg<sup>2+</sup> K<sup>+</sup> concentration as that in sea water (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). It can be seen that the as-prepared PB exhibits a comparable rate capability and cycling stability as SW-PB, suggesting that the impurities in sea water have limited effects on PB performance. In addition. we investigate the electrochemical performance of SW-PB washed by sea water to further show the influence of the impurities (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Note that the impurities in sea water would cause a negative impact on electrochemical performance with somewhat degraded cycle life and fluctuated coulombic efficiency, indicating an aggravated side reaction caused by the impurities. The above results suggest that the impurities in natural water indeed have adverse effects on electrochemical performance. But after DI water washing of the product, the influence of the impurities can be minimized. The electrochemical performance comparison of the above PB samples is summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Based on the Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> concentration in sea water, we further compare the electrochemical performance of PB samples synthesized in solutions with different Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> concentrations to obtain a proper Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping amount as shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16‒S19</a> (Supporting Information). The ionic concentration, materials composition and electrochemical performance are summarized in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). Of note is that the level of Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping in Na site is closely related to Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> concentration in the precursors. All the Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doped samples show enhanced cycling stability compared to DW-PB, suggesting that Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> co-doping indeed has a positive effect on framework stability but the degree of the enhancement effect varies with the doping level. The improvement degree for sample 1 with less or sample 4 with excessive Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> addition is lower than that of sample 2 and sample 3 with moderate Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> addition. Besides, a decrease in discharge capacity as well as rate capability is observed with excessive Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> addition. In a word, a strategy of moderate Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping can both enhance cycling stability and keep a high discharge capacity. Exactly, the Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> concentration in sea water is appropriate for moderate Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping, suggesting that the sea water can act as a reaction medium to achieve natural yet appropriate Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> doping in PB with improved electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <p>In order to understand the “pillar” effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> ions in PB, we investigated the electronic structure of the Na-PB, Mg<sup>−</sup>PB, and K-PB through DFT calculations as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information). The average transferred charges from Na to N, Mg to N, and K to N atom in Na-PB, Mg-PB, and K-PB framework are 0.85e, 1.69e, and 0.94e, respectively (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The increased charge transfer between Mg (or K) and N atoms indicates a stronger Coulombic attraction of Mg‒N and K‒N than Na‒N. The strongly bonded N atoms by Mg and K can well restrain the structural collapse after repetitive Na extraction/insertion which is well proved by the inhibited dissolution of the transition metal elements, where the Mn/Ni content of SW-PB after 300 cycles is obviously higher than that of DW-PB (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, we calculated the Bader charges variation on N atoms for Na-PB, Mg<sup>2+</sup> doped Na-PB and K<sup>+</sup> doped Na-PB during Na insertion/extraction process in order to well explain the framework stabilization effects of Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> doping. It can be seen that the existed Mg‒N, K‒N Coulombic interaction in PB framework can well reduce the charge density variation of the neighboring N atoms during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process, enabling a restrained Jahn-Teller effect of the central Mn atom (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15‒S17</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0047_R_d190594207e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Alloys Compd">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The well-maintained framework structure after Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> doping during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process is also proved by charge density difference as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). Clearly, there is negligible bonding variation of Mn‒N, N‒C, and C‒Fe during Na extraction/insertion process for Mg<sup>2+</sup> doped Na-PB and K<sup>+</sup> doped Na-PB, further demonstrating a better structural stability. The stabilizing mechanism of the PB framework by Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping is schematically presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0004">4b</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3c0729a1-2728-4918-be73-a8fad54e0586/advs9580-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3c0729a1-2728-4918-be73-a8fad54e0586/advs9580-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4890b838-da70-4329-b2c3-86900e77f764/advs9580-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3c0729a1-2728-4918-be73-a8fad54e0586/advs9580-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Optimized structures of Na-PB, Mg-PB, and K-PB and calculated charge transfer values from Na, Mg, and K to N atoms. b) Stabilizing mechanism of the PB framework by Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The impact of Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and phase transition was investigated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and in-situ XRD as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0005">5</a>. The average diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) values for DW-PB, SW-PB, LW-PB, and TW-PB at discharge process are 2.34×10<sup>‒10</sup>, 2.33×10<sup>‒10</sup>, 1.64×10<sup>‒10</sup>, 2.56×10<sup>‒10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively. It is evident that the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values are comparable, indicating that the trace Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> in the PB framework would not block the bulk Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Furthermore, the smaller particle size of SW-PB with Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> doping is beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. In-situ XRD suggests that SW-PB exhibits the similar structural evolution as DW-PB with reversible monoclinic-cubic-tetrahedral (Mon-Cub-Tet) and Tet-Cub-Mon phase transitions during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0004">4e,f</a>). As for the phase transition rate, it is clear that SW-PB has a slow structural variation from Cub to Tet phase than DW-PB (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0005">5e1‒e3,f1‒f3</a>). Notice that the Cub-Tet transform accounts for the main volume variation for the PB and the sluggish phase transform after Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping can relieve the structural changes which is good for enhanced cycling stability. To further understand the structure stabilizing effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> doping, the structural variation of SW-PB during charge-discharge process is investigated through XRD Rietveld refinements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) and the corresponding crystal parameters are summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). The results indicate that the crystal volume variation for SW-PB is only 0.17% after charge, which is far less than that of DW-PB (0.60%), suggesting a superior structure stabilizing effect of Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> co-doping. The electrochemically inert Mg<sup>2+</sup>,K<sup>+</sup> ions can act as “pillar” to well restrain phase transition and enhance structural stability as previously reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0048_R_d190594207e1567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">48</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0049_R_d190594207e1567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Small">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In a word, Mg<sup>2</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping in the PB can slow down structural changes during repetitive Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction because of the strong K‒N and Mg‒N interactions with stabilized crystal lattices.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b6d9e2cf-8b56-47ad-9571-d0ef7c89a68c/advs9580-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b6d9e2cf-8b56-47ad-9571-d0ef7c89a68c/advs9580-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/312fd252-d9be-4166-a87b-3ea49864a86d/advs9580-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b6d9e2cf-8b56-47ad-9571-d0ef7c89a68c/advs9580-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">GITT curves and the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values of a) SW-PB, b) DW-PB, c) LW-PB and d) TW-PB. 2D contour plots of in-situ XRD patterns of e) SW-PB and f) DW-PB during the initial charge/discharge process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to understand the role of Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping in maintaining the structural stability of the PB framework during cycling, the morphology evolution was checked after cycling as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information). Note that the majority of SW-PB particles can well maintain the initial cubic shape after 50, 150, and 300 cycles without severe particle dissolution and breach. By contrast, the particles of DW-PB, LW-PB, and TW-PB gradually become irregular with a rough surface after prolonged cycling, indicating a severe particle corrosion (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24‒S26</a>, Supporting Information). The morphology variation further demonstrates that the Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping endows the PB with a more rigid structure which can well tolerate repeated Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion and restrain the particle dissolution. It seems that the sea water can act as water source to synthesize high-performance PB by self-construction of Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping. Importantly, the strategy of direct use of sea water well fits the goal of large-scale, low -cost PB preparation and sustainable development.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs9580-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sustainable Preparation of PB using Recycled Salts</h3>
            
            <p>Considering the fact that the waste water after PB preparation contains substantial sodium salts (sodium citrate, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>), an attempt was made to directly use the waste water to prepare PB. The waste water was collected after the preparation of DW-PB as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information). The morphology, structure, and electrochemical performance of the PB prepared directly using waste water were compared with those of DW-PB. The results indicate that although the majority of the PB particles prepared with the waste water exhibit a large, regular cube morphology, there are some tiny and irregular particles, indicating an uncontrolled co-precipitation process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28a</a>, Supporting Information). The particle size distribution also shows an obviously reduced <i>D</i><sub>50</sub> (423&nbsp;nm), suggesting a quick co-precipitation reaction in the waste water (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28b</a>, Supporting Information). TG and XRD analyses show that the waste water sample still maintain a monoclinic structure but the crystal water content (12.54%) is increased compared to DW-PB (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28c,d</a>, Supporting Information). The chemical formula of the waste water sample is Na<sub>1.61</sub>Mn<sub>0.57</sub>Fe<sub>0.20</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.90</sub>·2.26H<sub>2</sub>O, which has an increased Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defect (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Water quality analysis shows that there are a large number of Mn, Fe, and Ni ions in the waste water (mainly Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and Ni ions), which would affect crystallization of the PB (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The waste water sample also shows a degraded electrochemical performance as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> (Supporting Information). The sample exhibits a reduced discharge capacity of 115.5 mAh g<sup>‒1</sup> at 0.1 C and deteriorated capacity retention of 88.7% at 20 C relative to that at 0.1 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29a‒c</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, when current density is back to 0.1 C, the discharge capacity cannot be well recovered, indicating a structural destruction after high-rate cycling. The sample also shows a relatively poor cycling stability with 82.8% retention after 500 cycles at 5 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29d</a>, Supporting Information). As a result, direct use of the waste water for PB synthesis is unfavorable and purification of the waste water is necessary.</p>
            
            <p>It should be noted that the composition of the waste water is rather complex containing sodium citrate, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4,</sub> and other residual ions such as Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4‒</sup>, Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and Ni<sup>2+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). Here, a simple purifying route is proposed by using precipitation and ion exchange route to remove the impurity ions in the waste water. The sodium citrate and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> were separated based on their solubility difference. XRD test reveals that the recycled sodium citrate and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> exhibit the almost same patterns as the pristine ones (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30b,c</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD results and the calculated recycling rate indicate that the proposed purifying method is highly efficient. ICP-AES results also demonstrate a high purity of the recycled salts solution (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The detailed process of the waste water purification and salts separation is shown in the Experimental Section and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information). Clearly, the morphology, structure, particle size, TG and chemical formulas of the PB using the recycled salts are comparable with those of DW-PB (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19–S22</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Electrochemical performance of the recycled salts PB sample is presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0006">6</a>. The initial capacity (116.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C), rate capability (89.6% at 20 C relative to that at 0.1 C) and cycle life (89.6% after 500 cycles at 5 C) of the PB are comparable with those of DW-PB (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The results suggest that the proposed recycling route is feasible and efficient. It also suggests trace amounts of impurity ions (&lt; 10&nbsp;mg L<sup>‒1</sup>) can hardly affect the crystallization of the PB because of the kinetically shielding effect of the high-concentration precursor salts. Note that sodium citrate and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> are the main costs for PB synthesis and the sodium salts recycling strategy would obviously reduce average raw materials cost (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). For example, the reuse of sodium citrate and the Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> for 9 times (total 10 times) can reduce the average raw materials cost by ≈40%.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9f845c13-29ac-40f4-abd0-d44a2737c23e/advs9580-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9f845c13-29ac-40f4-abd0-d44a2737c23e/advs9580-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3c122205-8c26-46cb-9d58-4a5b44d1d214/advs9580-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9f845c13-29ac-40f4-abd0-d44a2737c23e/advs9580-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the recycled salts sample. a) Voltage curves of the initial three cycles at 0.1 C. b) Discharge curves and c) discharge capacity at various current densities from 0.1 to 20 C, and d) cycling stability at 5 C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To highlight superior performance of our PB samples, we compare the electrochemical performance of SW-PB and the recycled salts sample with those of the previously reported Mn-based PB materials (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0007">7a,b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0015_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0023_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0032_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">32</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0034_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Mater. Today Sustain.">34</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0038_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0041_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">41</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0050_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Comm.">50</a>-<a href="#advs9580-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0055_R_d190594207e1804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Mater. Chem. C">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Clearly, the cycling stability and rate capability of SW-PB and the recycled salts sample are better than those of the other Mn-based PB materials, demonstrating feasibility of using sea water and recycled salts in PB synthesis. The self-constructed Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doping in Na sites can improve cycling stability because of the stronger Mg‒N and K‒N Coulombic attraction which can restrain the structure collapse and elements dissolution during repetitive Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. This work suggests that high-quality PB can be obtained by a cost-effective and sustainable route (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs9580-fig-0007">7c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs9580-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/24cce18d-7297-4231-bce3-345661da0d68/advs9580-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/24cce18d-7297-4231-bce3-345661da0d68/advs9580-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f9305929-16a8-4a4a-abea-fd2b24db9440/advs9580-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/24cce18d-7297-4231-bce3-345661da0d68/advs9580-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs9580-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202406842" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performance and b) rate performance comparison of SW-PB and the recycled salts sample with other Mn-based PB materials. c) Schematic illustration of sustainable synthesis of high-performance PB materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs9580-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs9580-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>A self-constructed trace Mg<sup>2+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> co-doped PB was synthesized in sea water which exhibits a superior rate performance (90.4% at 20 C relative to that at 0.1 C) and extremely long cycle life (80.1% retention after 2400 cycles at 5 C). The insertion of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> into PB is both thermodynamically and kinetically permitted during the sea water synthesis. DFT calculation reveals the “pillar” effect Mg<sup>2+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> which is essential to stabilize the PB framework. The enhanced Coulombic attraction of Mg‒N and K‒N pairs can relieve the phase transition and restrain the elements dissolution during repetitive Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. In addition, a feasible purification and separation strategy was proposed to recycle the sodium salts, so as to prepare PB in a cost-effective and sustainable route. This work proves that high performance and low production cost of PB can be simultaneously achieved which is critical for its practical applications in large-scale energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs9580-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs9580-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs9580-sec-0100-title"> Preparation of PB Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>The PB materials were synthesized via a chelating agent-assisted coprecipitation route.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0056_R_d190594207e1881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Mater. Chem. A">56</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0057_R_d190594207e1881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Energy Lett.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a typical synthesis in DI water, 4.8&nbsp;mmol of MnSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>H<sub>2</sub>O, 1&nbsp;mmol of FeSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>7H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.4&nbsp;mmol NiSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>6H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.02&nbsp;mol Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub><b>·</b>2H<sub>2</sub>O and 22.5&nbsp;g Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> were dissolved in 50&nbsp;mL of DI water to form solution A. 1.2&nbsp;mmol of MnSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>H<sub>2</sub>O, 1&nbsp;mmol of FeSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>7H<sub>2</sub>O, 1.6&nbsp;mmol NiSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>6H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.02&nbsp;mol Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub><b>·</b>2H<sub>2</sub>O and 5&nbsp;g Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> were dissolved in 50&nbsp;mL of DI water to form solution B. 10&nbsp;mmol of Na<sub>4</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<b>·</b>10H<sub>2</sub>O was dissolved in 100&nbsp;mL of DI water to form solution C. Both solution A and B were bubbled with N<sub>2</sub> flow to avoid oxidation of divalent iron ions. Then, solution B and C were added dropwise into solution A simultaneously through a peristaltic pump under magnetic stirring at 60&nbsp;°C in an oil bath. The feeding speed of solution B and C was 0.5&nbsp;mL min<sup>‒1</sup> and 1&nbsp;mL min<sup>‒1</sup>, respectively. After the reaction, the white suspension was aged for 3&nbsp;h under stirring with N<sub>2</sub> flow at 60&nbsp;°C. The white precipitate was separated and washed by centrifugation in DI water and absolute alcohol. Finally, the PB material was obtained after drying at 110&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h in vacuum (labeled as DW-PB). Similarly, lake water, sea water and tap water instead of DI water were used to synthesize PB materials with a same process which were labeled as LW-PB, SW-PB and TW-PB, respectively. The lake water is from Qiandao Lake (Hangzhou), the sea water is from Yintan Beach (Weihai), and the tap water is from Zhejiang University (Hangzhou). All of the other chemical reagents for synthesis were purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd (analytical reagents). The synthesis method for PB samples with different Mg<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> contents is similar to that for SW-PB except that the sea water was replaced by DI water with a desirable amount of MgCl<sub>2</sub> and KCl.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs9580-sec-0110-title"> Na Salts Recycling and Reuse</h4>
                  
                  <p>The waste water was first concentrated to 110&nbsp;mL in an oil bath at 95&nbsp;°C followed by vacuum suction filtration. The preparation process of the PB material using the waste water is the same as DW-PB except that DI water in solution A and B was replaced by the waste water (57&nbsp;mL in solution A and 53&nbsp;mL in solution B) without using Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>. The preparation of solution C is the same as DW-PB by using DI water as the solvent. After the reaction, the PB was separated and washed by centrifugation in DI water and absolute alcohol followed by drying at 110&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h in vacuum. The separation of Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> from the waste water adopts a simple solubility difference strategy and the impurity ions were removed through precipitation processes assisted by ion exchange using an ion-exchange resin (Purolite, S930Plus). The detained separation process is presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information). Specifically, 0.01&nbsp;mol CuSO<sub>4</sub><b>·</b>5H<sub>2</sub>O was first added into 100&nbsp;mL of the waste water with heating at 95&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h to remove the residual Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4‒</sup>. Then, the suspension was suction filtered and 0.03&nbsp;mol NaOH was added into the filtrate to remove the Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Ni<sup>2+</sup>, and Cu<sup>2+</sup>. After sufficient reaction at 95&nbsp;°C and suction filtration, 0.1&nbsp;mol L<sup>‒1</sup> H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> was added into the filtrate dropwise to adjust the solution pH. The obtained solution was further purified by using ion-exchange resin to adsorb the remaining ions (mainly Cu<sup>2+</sup>) in a chromatography column. After that, the solution was directly concentrated to 15&nbsp;mL at 95&nbsp;°C and cooled to 5&nbsp;°C to crystallize Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> followed by suction filtration. Finally, the filtrate was heated at 95&nbsp;°C with water evaporation to obtain Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O. The obtained Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub> were washed with 95% alcohol solution to further remove the impurity ions and the drying was conducted in a vacuum oven at 110&nbsp;°C. The preparation of the recycled salts sample is the same as DW-PB by using recycled Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs9580-sec-0120-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Crystal structure of the PB materials was characterized by XRD on a powder diffractometer (Rigaku D/Max-2550&nbsp;pc) with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.541 Å). TG tests were performed in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere on a Netzsch LFA467 device. Chemical composition of the PB materials and ion concentration of the various water sources were determined using ICP-AES (Agilent 720ES system). Morphology of the samples was characterized by field-emission SEM (Hitachi SU8010 microscope). Size distribution of the PB materials was determined by Zeta potential analysis (Malvern Zetasizer Nano ZS90). The microstructure of the PB was analyzed by TEM on a Tchnai F20 microscope.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs9580-sec-0130-title"> In-Situ XRD Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>In-situ XRD tests were conducted on a Rigaku MiniFlex 600. To increase the peak intensity of the XRD patterns and eliminate peaks of the current collector, a free standing electrode was made by pressing active material and carbon nanotube (CNT) into a pellet at mass ratio of 7:3 under 4&nbsp;MPa. The cathode pallet was placed in contact with Beryllium window followed by adding separator (glass fiber), electrolyte (1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC): fluoroethylene (FEC), 9:1 in volume), and sodium metal anode in sequence. The fabrication of the in-situ cell was conducted in an Ar-filled glove box. During in-situ XRD measurement, the cell was charged and discharged at 0.1 C (1 C = 150&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and the patterns were collected every 15&nbsp;min to capture the structural evolution of the cathode materials.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs9580-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">CR2025-type coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox, using Na metal disk as anode, glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman) as separator, and an electrolyte containing 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) and PC (1:1 in volume) with 4 wt.% FEC additive (DoDoChem). For the preparation of cathode, 70 wt.% active material, 10 wt.% polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), and 20% carbon nanotubes (CNT) were mixed in N-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) to form a slurry. The slurry was pasted on Al foil followed by drying under vacuum at 110&nbsp;°C for 24&nbsp;h. Charge and discharge cycling of the coin cells was conducted on a battery testing system (Neware Technology Ltd, China) at room temperature over a voltage range of 2–4&nbsp;V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). GITT was used to measure the Na<sup>+</sup> chemical diffusion coefficients using a current pulse of 15&nbsp;mA g<sup>‒1</sup> for 10&nbsp;min and relaxation time for 1&nbsp;h until a desired cut-off voltage was reached. Before the GITT measurements, the cells were charged and discharged at 15&nbsp;mA g<sup>‒1</sup> for three cycles. Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated by the following equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0058_R_d190594207e2135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">58</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0059_R_d190594207e2135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>:
                     <div class="inline-equation" id="advs9580-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs9580-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs9580-sec-0150-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the calculations were performed by applying the spin-polarized DFT method using the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP) with the projector-augmented wave (PAW) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0060_R_d190594207e2232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Phys. Rev. B">60</a>, <a href="#advs9580-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0061_R_d190594207e2232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Phys. Rev. B">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) with Perdew-Burke-and Ernzerhof (PBE) was used for the exchange-correlation functional. In order to simplify the calculation model, the higher symmetry Mn-PB framework was adopted to replace the Ni,Fe doped Mn-PB framework. The Hubbard U values (<i>U</i><sub>eff</sub>) of 5.0&nbsp;eV for Mn and 3.0&nbsp;eV for Fe were set to describe the d-part of the Hamiltonian based on the previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs9580-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs9580-bib-0062_R_d190594207e2240" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Chem. Mater.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The plane-wave energy cutoff was set above 400&nbsp;eV. For structure optimization, the ions force convergence and the electronic energy convergence criteria were set to 0.05&nbsp;eV Å<sup>‒1</sup> and 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV, respectively. A 3×3×3 Monkhorst-Pack k-point mesh was used for the Brillouin zones.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The reversible insertion reaction of the alkali cations A (A represents Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, and Ca<sup>2+</sup>) into MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and relative insertion energy can be written as follows:

                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{\mathrm{MnFe}}{{\left( {{\mathrm{CN}}} \right)}_6} + {\mathrm{A}} \to {\mathrm{AMnFe}}{{\left( {{\mathrm{CN}}} \right)}_6}, {{E}_{i1}} &amp;=&amp; E\left[ {{\mathrm{AMnFe}}\left( {{\mathrm{CN}}} \right)} \right]\nonumber\\ &amp;&amp;- E\left[ {{\mathrm{MnFe}}{{{\left( {{\mathrm{CN}}} \right)}}_6}} \right] - E\left[ {\mathrm{A}} \right]\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
